# Hark on the Wire

We are given the packet capture [hark_on_the_wire.pcapng](./)

## Part 1 - Analyzing The Packet Capture

The packet capture we are given is full of quite a lot of noise and packets that are meaningless to us:

![](./_images/packet_capture.png)

Luna used an analysis tool in order to find some particularly relevant packets close to the end of the file.
They are a series of plain-text packets sent between `127.0.0.1` and `127.0.0.1` that appear to contain a conversation
and some binary data:

![](./_images/packet_relevant.png)

We can use a quick python script using the `pyshark` library to print out the data from each of these packets:

[1_get_packets.py](./1_get_packets.py)

```py
import pyshark

cap = pyshark.FileCapture("./hark_on_the_wire.pcapng")
for packet in cap:
    # Skip non-IP packets and non-TCP packets
    if not hasattr(packet, 'ip'):
        continue
    if not hasattr(packet, 'tcp'):
        continue

    # Skip TCP packets that don't push any data
    if packet.tcp.flags_push == 'False':
        continue

    if packet.ip.src == '127.0.0.1':
        # packet.tcp.payload is formatted as a hex-string separated by ":"
        payload = [int(x, 16) for x in packet.tcp.payload.split(":")]
        print(bytes(payload))

```

This gives us the following output:

```
b'DONE'
b'Emma: Did you get your paper done for Professor Mitchell\xe2\x80\x99s class?'
b"Josh: Yeah, barely. I was up until like 3 AM working on that. I don't even want to think about Colonial Virginia history for a while."
b"Emma: Same. I thought it'd be easier, but she\xe2\x80\x99s intense! I was writing about the House of Burgesses, and it just spiraled into way more research than I planned."
b'Josh: Ugh, I did mine on early plantation systems. So much reading. But, hey, at least we survived.'
b'Emma: True! I still can\xe2\x80\x99t believe how fast this semester\xe2\x80\x99s flying by though. Feels like just yesterday we were doing the Yule Log ceremony.'
b"Josh: Yeah, I think so. It's always fun seeing everyone pack into the Wren Yard. Plus, free food at Sadler after."
b'Emma: Tell me about it. My Philosophy 101 class is killing me. The readings are so dense! Sometimes I wonder if I accidentally signed up for a grad-level course.'
b'Josh: Don\xe2\x80\x99t remind me! Midterms are coming up way too fast. I\xe2\x80\x99ve barely started prepping for my Computer Science exam.'
b'Emma: Yup! I\xe2\x80\x99m looking forward to the parade and the tailgate. Are you doing anything with your club for it?'
b'Josh: Philosophy\xe2\x80\x99s tough. I can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine. But hey, at least we have Homecoming soon. That\xe2\x80\x99ll be a nice break.'
b'Emma: Same! I just hope it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t rain like last year.'
b'Josh: Yeah, the Debate Society is doing a booth. I\xe2\x80\x99m supposed to help set it up. Should be fun, but I\xe2\x80\x99m more excited about the football game.'
b'Emma: Fingers crossed. Alright, I\xe2\x80\x99ve gotta run to my Government class. I\xe2\x80\x99ll see you at Sadler later?'
b'Josh: I checked. Should be sunny. We\xe2\x80\x99ll be good!'
b'Emma: Thanks, I\xe2\x80\x99ll need it! [laughs]'
b'Josh: Definitely. Good luck with the reading!'
b'Emma: Did you get the new plans for ISC 4?'
b'Josh: Yes, I did. I already sent them.'
b'Emma: Good.'
b'Josh: Remember, the key is TheSecretOfTheCrimDell'
```

Interesting. The conversation at the end seems fairly self-explanatory, we're looking for ISC 4 plans that are supposed to be
encrypted with the `TheSecretOfTheCrimDell`.
Before this, we see some binary strings that start with the name of a file in ASCII, and end with the string `DONE`.

## Part 2 - Downloading the Database

We can guess that these binary strings encode the ISC4 plans referenced in the conversation.
Let's make some changes to our python script to download these:

```py
import os.path

import pyshark

if not os.path.exists("output"):
    os.mkdir("output")

output_file = None
expecting_filename = False

cap = pyshark.FileCapture("./hark_on_the_wire.pcapng")
for packet in cap:
    # Skip non-IP packets and non-TCP packets
    if not hasattr(packet, 'ip'):
        continue
    if not hasattr(packet, 'tcp'):
        continue

    # Skip TCP packets that don't push any data
    if packet.tcp.flags_push == 'False':
        continue

    if packet.ip.src == '127.0.0.1':
        # packet.tcp.payload is formatted as a hex-string separated by ":"
        payload = [int(x, 16) for x in packet.tcp.payload.split(":")]
        print(bytes(payload))

        if output_file is not None:
            if bytes(payload).endswith(b'DONE'):
                output_file.write(bytes(payload).strip(b'DONE'))
                output_file.close()
                output_file = None
            else:
                output_file.write(bytes(payload))

        elif expecting_filename:
            output_file = open("output/" + bytes(payload).decode('ascii'), "wb")
            expecting_filename = False

        else:
            # Each file transfer starts with a valid integer
            try:
                int(bytes(payload).decode('ascii'))
                expecting_filename = True
            except ValueError:
                continue
```

As expected, this gives us three output files.
This file structure is typical of an SQLite database, but they can't be opened with plain SQLite.
After some research, it seems like they have been encoded following the [SQLCipher Format](https://github.com/sqlcipher/sqlcipher).

## Part 3 - Decrypting the Database

There is a convenient python library we can use to open such files: `sqlite3-binary`

[3_decrypt_database.py](./3_decrypt_database.py)

```py
import sqlcipher3

conn = sqlcipher3.connect("output/wm_encrypted_db.db")
cursor = conn.cursor()

# Authenticate the database with the key from the conversation
cursor.execute("PRAGMA key = 'TheSecretOfTheCrimDell'")

# Find all tables
database_tables = []
cursor.execute("SELECT name FROM sqlite_master WHERE type='table';")
for (name,) in cursor.fetchall():
    database_tables.append(name)
    print("Table found", name)

# Print the contents of each table
for table in database_tables:
    cursor.execute(f"SELECT * FROM {table}")
    print("Contents of table", table)
    for row in cursor.fetchall():
        print(row)
```

![](./_images/flag.png)

And there is our flag! Pwned/QED
